RATING: 7


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Area control that involves influencing (getting majorities) people in order to receive the rewards they offer.

Ganeplay is through the use of cards, which introduces an element of luck, but this is mitigated by having an extra card to play each round.  Dual use of the cards (play influence or re-stock) is a nice touch, as is the multiple ways to score (completed missions, victory chips), and the use of money.

There are only 4 rounds, therefore playtime is reasonably quick, it plays very well with 2-4 players (I was surprised that the 2-player with the dummy player works so well), it has minimal luck, and its a cheap purchase.  Overall, despite the thin theme, the game works and is more than the sum of its parts, and I would recommend it, and note that it is one of the most requested games by my wife and my more casual gaming friends, despite the fact that it's not a casual game.

Note: make sure to laminate and/or get card protectors for the cards, as they are very cheaply made.

Own the [boardgame=165351]Favourite[/boardgame] mini-expansion from the [boardgame=40688]Treasure Chest[/boardgame].
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Interesting worker placement in which going last in turn order is a huge advantage. On your turn you play a card from your hand and can place 1-3 workers on that tile. Then you may move workers to an adjacent tile (as long as you leave one on the starting tile) and if you want you may move the workers again to an adjacent tile (as long as you leave a worker behind). Play continues like this until everyone has played all but one of their cards. If you had the most workers on a tile, you are awarded the token from the tile [you need these tokens to activate and score your mission cards]. If you did not have the most people, you have the option sometimes to pay for the token.

My first play was a 2 player game and it moved fairly quickly, but I could imagine the game taking much longer the more people are playing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
There are quite a lot of rules, subrules and sub-subrules for tiebreaker compared to what Louis XIV is, a middleweight, tactical eurogame. Long setup, extremely procedural and unintuitive gameplay and the ice on the cake is the endgame scoring for the majority of each type of coat of arms which injects an absolutely unnecessary luck element into the game. I did not feel for a moment that this mess was taken place in the court of Loius XIV, except for the baroque-ish rulebook (worst from alea). 

And the strangest thing, that contrary to all of these concerns I rather enjoy the game with 2p. However, because of the rules (and how they are written) I will hardly introduce the game for my casual gamer friends. Mostly tactical game, although it can reward some strategic planning.

RATING: 5.3
A good and enjoyable game, which is quite simple to play after you know the rules. However the game makes this a punishing experience. The iconography and labels are inconsistent and mostly meaningless (you are *constantly* referring to manual for clarifications), the theme could be better, the way features are scored is counter-intuitive, it's difficult for new players to understand the difference between different supplies (general/personal), the objective and player achievement is not obvious, and many of the mission cards are misleadingly worded.Apart from that though, a very good game!
Keeps dropping points after more plays...

RATING: 7
A really fun game.  There's a sharp balance between strategy (how & which cards to play, intrigue cards) and randomness (card draw, shields) at work.  A fair amount of player interaction during the influence phase as players compete for the various spots.  The theme is well integrated with the game play and works nicely.  I would gladly play this one again.  Rating may improve with additional plays.

I got the sense that the random aspects could overwhelm the strategic ones, particularly when it comes down to the wire.  From one play, it's difficult to get a good feel for this balance.

RATING: 5
Although this game has a nice theme and great components, and plays through adequately, the game itself I find to be unenjoyable. I'm really interested to discover why many people have rated it so highly. 

I have had 3 plays now and have'nt discovered why I might want to play again. The rules are a bit confusing to understand in the English version, but are comprehensive; you just need to go through them carefully to understand the game mechanics because the game is not intutive in play. 

I played through this game once solitare( 2 player) to learn the game. On the surface the strategic choice in the game appears wide and complex, but my solitare expierience showed me how many game choices were severely limited by the influence cards in your hand. So the overall effect seemed to be a fairly random game. 

The greatest problem with the game is that the cause/effect game play processes are confused by there being too many variables, game stages, pieces (many bits & no board!) and proceedures to move/allocate which make the game hang together in a very roundabout way (but its kind of fun anyway). 

It will be interesting to see if many are interested in playing this game repeatedly.....(I might have missed something)

RATING: 7
Louis manages to pack quite a lot of elements into a pretty small package; there's resource management (influence pieces and money), time management (getting those crucial end of round placements by picking up influence cards, intrigue cards, and through certain personalities)and a strategic decision about how much attention to pay to the shields.  In addition to managing those resources, there's a need to pick your fights carefully.  I've played with both three and four; with three it seems to lack something.

RATING: 7.5
{2 plays, 4 player} It's really just a bunch of old systems from other games, but mixed in a nice enough way and given enough of a twist to seem somewhat new and fresh.  So it was interesting, but... far too long and fiddley for what it is.  It seemed fiddley when it was taught to me, and then I tried to teach the next game, and, man, there are too many little rules here. After more play... [original rating: 7]

Well, dang, if my opinion hasn't gone up a little after, believe it or not, a 3-player game.  I was worried there'd be less conflict, but not only was there plenty of conflict, you could actually target other players better (you no longer had to hit the 4th place player because it was the best move for the cards you had).  Those Alea people know something.

RATING: 9
My favorite area-majority game.

The game board is really interesting with each spot offering a unique but valuable bonus.  But you are dealt cards that limit where you can play.  Each card lets you play on the space it represents as well as neighboring spaces.

And, of course, the trick is to win majorities with little competition.  You want to avoid getting stuck piling your resources into one space in competition with another player.

The unique bonuses of each space, coupled with the limited -- but still numerous -- options presented by your cards, weighed against were your opponents are playing make for some really great decisions.

We've had fun with this with less than the ideal number of 4 players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
given what little this adds to the majority space, i'm kinda surprised at the high ratings.  there are some interesting mechanics in play, but the action is on the high end of the fiddly scale for a euro.  it did blow by in a pleasant amount of time, much less than the cited 75-100m although i wasn't timing it.  at 4 rounds, perhaps too short due to effectively random screwage.  but since player powers are developed throughout the game, i think it's unlikely that people will mount a big comeback if they're behind on 2  cards.  i was also surprised at how little the dreaded and discussed random shield thing ended up affecting the game.  i could see it in very close games, but are they usually?  also, for a game that is thematically void i think that something more fun or exciting than this could have been chosen.

RATING: 8
Perhaps the best area control game out there. 
+ The tiles flip over when activated, thus changing the game
+ You don't always need the majority on a tile because the first player always loses his cubes, the others on the tile can purchase the item on the tile.
+ Variant in the file section so you can enjoy this game with 2 players
+ The "leave a trail" system: the movement of your cubes needs to follow a certain pattern

- Randomness of the cards

RATING: 9
A board game without board. Players in 4 turns try to score victory points realizing missions. These can be accomplished winning over 12 personage from Louis XIV court, who provide each one a different advantage. Accomplished missions provide also a bonus or a special ability of some sort.
The game is much more complex than this brief description.
The interaction between character bonus, mission bonus and players strategy should guarantee to this title a long life.
As usual in this kind of influence game, efficiency is the key.
There is a random element stronger than in Goa but much less important than players decisions. Player interaction is quite high, and there is also much confrontation. A fun meaty game

RATING: 7
This game can be rather difficult to understand from just reading the rules. Even after a good explanation players usually sit around with blank stares on their faces. This is mostly due to the very abstract nature of the game. The theme does not really connect with the gameplay. However the gameplay is really superb. Lot's of intracacies and the need to spend your influence wisely can make this into a brain burner. But the random card distribution adds the need for more tactical play and saves the game from a.p. hell. The end game scoring is a bit too random for me and I would like some variant where you have more influence on which coat of arms you get. Overall a challenging euro that plays pretty fluent once you get the hang of it.

RATING: 9
My favorite Modular Board game.  Elegant in every respect.  I recommend that you use some of the file downloads offered on BGG for teaching this game.

Player Aid: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/fileinfo.php?fileid=15465

Outstanding player aid to assist with evaluating tiles. Confused about ties? Can't remember when pieces go to the general supply vs. the private supply? This is the player aid you need. I've never played a game without it. Absolutely speeds up the whole process. It's also very attractively designed. I mounted them on posterboard and plasticized them.

http://www.bggfiles.com/viewfile.php3?fileid=10106

A single sheet (when printed double sided) that summarises everything from setup, the four phases of each turn, including a single table for resolving the influence phase, as well as end game scoring.

RATING: 6
Long & complex.  Limited control.  Plenty of options during a turn, but nothing you do has much certainty.  So much hidden information (influence cards, intrigue cards & secret mission cards) that you just don't know enough about what your oppenents want... nor do you know what they are capable of doing.  The game can develop a boom and bust cycle if players insist on making the bulk of the courtiers flip in unison (i.e. causing most of the courtiers to have the same winning condition for a given round, then all having the opposite for the next round).  I wasn't pleased with the "hand management" portion of the game.  Getting dealt multiple wild cards can really hurt a player (house rules could fix this).  Also, you must play 4 of 5 cards each round and you don't carry cards over between rounds... so this is tactics-heavy (low strategy) game.  The shield scoring at the end adds randomness, but I think it is balanced by the high value of completed secret mission cards.  Multiple paths to victory is a good thing, and I won a game by collecting shields and finishing just an average number of secret missions.

** UPDATE: the game is mostly tactical, but I enjoy it (probably because of the secret mission cards).  You must play somewhat conservatively in order to be sure to collect the guaranteed items, but you must also take risks and try to win majorities because you cannot win by only working on the "safe" nobles.

Others say:  Not good with 3 players.  Two fight and the third gets what he wants.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Louis XIV is a good example of what happens to what might be a decent little game when it's buried underneath layers of excessive complexity and fiddliness.

At its heart, it's a simple area-control game... players try to influence the members of the court, each of which has some sort fo unique game benefit. Unfortunately, the designer didn't stop there.

TIles are flipped, but only under certain conditions ("give me the rulebook"). Influence markers are divided, El Grande-style, into two seperate groups, though it is never made clear what the two groups are in terms of the theme, and once an action is calculated some influence markers go to the general supply and others to your own supply ("pass the rulebook"). Different tiles have different tiebreaking methods ("hand me the rulebook again"). And the final scoring method was just weird and complicated.

DOn't get me wrong... I enjoy complex games, but there needs to be some method to the madness. BUt Louis XIV struck me, i nthe words of gamethyme, as "complex for the sake of complexity."
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Fun game of area control. Lots of tactical choices as players deploy their influence tokens to gain the advantages and thus favors (special abilities/actions) of the various characters. RATING: 10
12/25/05 - 6+ plays so far. Really enjoying this game and it is great when my wife likes these games too. Bribing the individuals is usually a good move but you need to be careful that you have enough money to bribe them all so that you can get your influence markers back in your supply. Otherwise, you'll end up trying to win their influence - thereby losing your influence markers and making it difficult to compete in the next round. Wonderful tension exisits throughout the game.

RATING: 7
Pretty area majority type game where you place your dudes in order to get you various rewards from the king's court.

There's a balance between satisfying your cards, which gain you points and special abilities, and gaining shields, which get you end of game points.  Both seem fairly important, as you're likely not going to win with a good dose of both!

It's also a tough decision deciding how many guys are worth committing to a specific reward, and when to decide when someone just plain wants it worse than you do, saving the guys for something else.

Overall, a neat little game that is fairly simple and plays rather quickly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Having lived for a good number of years in Versailles and acquired an interest in Bourbon history, I was eager to try this one out. I'm a big fan of Im Schatten des Kaisers - a majorities game doing a great job portraying medieval politics and intrigue - and was hoping for a game bringing 17th/18th century court intrigue to the table.

However, it has to be said that the theme is only about as strong as say that of Saint Petersburg. Nice touches here and there, but the game could have easily been about 20th century intrigue in the Kremlin or the like. That said, the graphics - based on famous old masters paintings - is terrific and helps a great deal in creating the right atmosphere.

One playing clearly was not enough to explore this game's depth and viable strategies. I like what I see a good deal, but it hasn't blown me away as Im Schatten des Kaisers's greatness has.

One thing I did not like, was the coats of arms chips' bonus being entirely arbitrary. There is no control whatsoever regarding that aspect of the game. You're subject to the whims of Fortuna. Considering that the COA chips' bonus is merely one extra VP, that may not seem much, but the final scores in our four-player game were very close. 40-46 range. I wish there'd be some strategic depth to the Coats of Arms, which it is IMO sorely lacking. We're already discussing more strategic variant approaches.

Really, I think a golden opportunity to enhance the theme was also lost on the coats of arms count. It would have been great if those rewards would grant you duchies, posts in the civil service, court hierarchy and armed forces. That's after all what the French nobility craved for.

The game system is intriguing, but it appears to me that Rüdiger Dorn should have gone that extra mile to make it more strategic and much truer to its theme.

I see why it reminds some of Ys. Fortunately, it is much more to my liking - whereas Ys did nothing for me.

RATING: 6
Granted I've only played once...but in this case I think that was enough. 

I enjoyed the game quite a bit. It certainly felt as fulfilling as Goa or Jambo. Dorn has been really hitting some solid extra-base hits with his last few offerings. I thought the theme, the game play, the pacing....it all just works very well. Much like Goa, you are using various means to get an economy generating points. Trying to use your precious actions efficiently.

BUT THE SHIELDS! How can such a fun and tense game be decided by something so arbitrary as the face down shield tokens. In my one playing, myself and another player were tied for the lead and both had the same number of shields. I won because I got one bonus point...through dumb luck. 

I'm afraid the shield mechanic just spoils it for me. I suspect there is a decent variant or something else that can be done to mitigate this effect, but out of the box....I don't think I'll be needing to play it any more. Winning or losing on such a blatant luck-play just leaves a horrible taste in my mouth. I just don't understand.....why?!

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
alea is back on track. Dorn again takes bits of his older games, and then grafts them onto an interesting new system and successfully makes another gamer's game and further establishes himself as a top level designer. Louis incorporates both longer term planning (fufilling missions) amd tactical play (making your cards work). I like the piece placement scheme which borrows from Grande (must get pieces out of a central pool before you can place them) and Traders (The tower: pieces placed in Louis can ,,walk" to diagonal squares, leaving trails). The missions are also nifty in that the special powers they confer allow you to tailor your game. I do have to nitpick though, this simply does not play as well with 3 because the player order becomes even more important than usual and is unbalanced with one player getting to go last twice. The two and four player games both seem to work very well though, with 4 of course being the best, but this still scales down to 2 incredibly well for an area influence game, which rather surprised me. The game also has the virtue of playing pretty quickly once people are familiar with it, and having a large number of interesting decisions, but it also has a number of elements that are out of your control as well, possibly an uncomfortable number of them for fans of heavier games.

Louis has ultimately ended up losing points from me with repeat play though. The mission cards are the main variable factor in this game, and once one plays the game a handful of times, they also start to become retreads. The fact that they come into one's hand randomly is necessary to keep the system on track (players pursuing different chits, and not sure which ones beyond the current round / cards not balanced) but it also seems to lower the replay value since all one can do is play to one's hand as well as possible. This isn't the only game with that factor, but I think Louis suffers a bit for it. I also have grown to dislike the fiddle factor of the setup, which probably just means that the game is growing stale for me, because it didn't bother me much when I first sat down to it. I think the bottom line is that Louis is fresh for a number of plays, but that it just didn't have enough in the tank to keep me captivated beyond that number. Not bad at all, would play it, but doubt that I'll suggest it again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In the beginning I wasn't too thrilled about Louis XIV. It took me ages to finally place an order with Adam Spielt (now defunct, unfortunately), and the first sessions left me with a splitting headache from absorbing the rules. The game isn't that difficult when shown how to play, but learning from the rule book requires quite a lot of reading. However, I persevered despite being shown every corner of the board(s?) by my main gaming partner, and now, after many games, it has become a title I would not easily part with.

Louis XIV is a funky excercise in majority gaming where big kahunas of the Sun King's court (i.e., Louis XIV) are bribed, bought and sucked up to for various goodies which can be used to complete 'missions'—basically ways to gain even more influence and money. Subject-wise this link is a bit weak, but it works nonetheless. The cool thing is that once a kahuna has given out a reward, (s)he changes the conditions for receiving that reward in the next round. This gives the game an incredibly dynamic feel, as there are many ways in which the players can achieve their goal, while being curtailed by the others. Delicious stuff.

Randomness? Yes, there is randomness. First in the cards you play to decide where you place influence. However, I've noticed that you can 'work around' a seemingly ill draw by focusing your attention elsewhere instead of trying to stick to your guns. Bend is the operative verb here, not break. The second source deals with the coats of arms. But these aren't as random as you might think. Of the 40+ points I score nowadays, only 2 to 4 are there by pure chance. And it is not like you don't know there will be 6 bonus points handed out at the end, so you'd best work you ass off to make sure those bonus points will not cause a nasty surprise! Things can get close, but they would have been close anyway, especially given the proficiency of the players in my group. The fun is more in [i]how[/i] you got to where you are, not [i]where[/i] you are. That makes it less of an issue for me, too.

Although I appreciate Louis XIV's design much more than in the beginning, grown to like and perhaps even love it too, I still slightly prefer the clean sober play of Mykerinos over the rock'n'roll of Louis when it comes to relatively short area majority games for 2 to 4 players. I am very happy to own both, though, and will play both with equal enjoyment and interest. For those interested: I play the 2P game with two variants suggested here on BGG. First, a passive 3rd and 4th player are added (2 influence for player 3, 1 influence for player 4). This crowds the board sufficiently to keep things interesting. But for ultimate fun, you need live dummies.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.9305
4.2.12 I played this game yesterday with high hopes and was not disappointed. Reading through the rules this game reminded me a tiny bit of Kingsburg but is far superior is my opinion. In this game you use influence markers and place them on different spaces. In most cases if you have the most influence on the space you get the benefit for free. If you have some influence on a space you may be able to buy the benefit or if you have a certain number receive it for free. So you are trying to get the most benefit with the least amount of influence. 

At the end of each round you can turn in special tokens you've picked up to gain special benefits for the rest of the game. 

I quite like this game and trying to get the resources you need and hoping someone else doesn't beat you out. Quite possibly my all time second favorite game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is another game in the genre of "not being able to do everything you want to" games.  Players use their influence to move amongst nobles to curry favor - but they need particular favors to complete missions and thus make their life easier.  Area control is the name of the game, as only the majority player on each noble's board gets an added benefit.

Perhaps a bit too complex for its own good, but there is an enjoyable game here.  Invest in teaching it to a few friends and then bring it back out with them again.

Not a very good game with 2 players, but works well with 4.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I like most of this game. I feel like it can hurt way too much to do poorly in an early round, though, and fall behind on the missions - you take a hit on points *and* powers. And since people choose what to pursue based mostly on what they need, where they can play, and what's an easy target, there's not much help in "others choosing not to compete as hard with you". It's not [i]always[/i] a problem, but in perhaps 50% of the games I've played, at least one player has suffered from it.

[10/09: Played a game recently where this was very much not the case; the early leader came in a distant 2nd. Unsure if it was random chance, that there were only 3 players, or some other factor.]

RATING: 5
Components: Sturdy components, so-so iconography, attractive portraits (though they're all upside down for some players). I wish this had come with a board. The game feels very scattered, and could have benefited with board art to unify the components and enhance the theme. All that need vary on the board are thin tiles that would flip beside each portrait.

Experience: 2014-01-22: Eight and a half years later, and how my tune has changed. Dropping my rating from a 7 to a 5. There are many great mechanisms to like here: variable income; strategic options in playing my influence cards; gaining rewards; building a tableau of powers; flipping the used board tiles. These elements just don't seem to synergize well. Lots of moving pieces that feel like I'm just playing Factory Fun with a different theme: get these two inputs to make this output, which I combine with some other input, to get yet another output. Very fiddly. I'm not drawn to the theme.

2005-06-04: The central mechanism of playing a card and placing pieces on the matching portrait, or to connected portraits, is great; it gives me something to shoot for during the round. After one playing, I dislike the powers associated with the mission cards, because a) it's difficult to keep track of the capabilities of other players, and b) success begets success, making it difficult to catch up.

First Played: 2005-06-04
Last Played: 2014-01-22
Plays (2):
2 players: 1
4 players: 1

RATING: 7
Very good game.   In one of my first three games the winner was determined by the blind draw of shields.  After all the chances for strategy and intrigue (yes, the game title is accurate) in the placement phases of the game, to have it end up being determined by who chose the best face down tokens seemed like sort of a let down.  After playing the game several more times I have noted that the random shield draw has been a factor in determining who wins the game more often than I am comfortable with in a strategy game.  I'm also noticing that some of the mission cards are much more powerful than others, adding to the luck of the draw being influential in determining the outcome of the game.  Finally, despite what others have said online, we have found the game to be virutally unplayable with three players, almost always resulting in one player being hopelessly out of contention by the end of the second turn.Despite all this, I still rate the game a 7, but that's with four players and carries with it the caveat that there's a lot of potential for random elements to play a major role in determining the winner.

UPDATE (5/06): Dropped from 9 to 8. As a matter of fact, now Louis falls in the same class of Maharaja, i.e., games with lots of rules and lots of chaos, and I know that this is a combination which appeals to very few players.

RATING: 6
Players are a faction of nobles trying to influence powerful personages in the court of the Sun King.  Okay... players basically play numbered cards that allow influence markers to be sprinkled over the persons, who are layed out in checker-board fashion on the table.  Influence can be jumped to diagonally adjacent persons, in Ruediger Dorn style (see Goa and Traders of Genoa).  Influence gains reward, and reward is spent on missions, and missions pay the highest return of victory points (as well as allow game breaking powers).  All good meaty stuff in a nice small box.  Early days, and lots more to explore here.  Another feather in Alea's cap.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4

RATING: 5
Abbreviated El Grande. Lackluster but decent.

Lacking a lot in the theme department - couldn't they have just added a little something to ground you? Sprinkling words like "intrigue, blackmail, seduction, and bribery" would go a long way to making me care about the movement of cubes around a board. And bylines for the people - I'm not going to have everyone I teach the game to read the historical notes in the rules. Putting the words "shady Treasurer" or "King's mistress" really really make people feel that they are doing something that matters more than scoring points. And the "special powers" you earn, c'mon, Give it a Name!! What are these, they're not missions, they're favors and kickbacks you're earning on behalf of provincials and minor nobles. I've hardly ever seen such a natural shoo-in for theme be so blunderingly avoided by Alea at all costs.

RATING: 5
The spiritual precursor to Notre Dame.   Some elements are exactly the same:  playing cards to determine where your active influence is placed, and the need to activate more of your influence markers from reserves.   This game is a bit more spatial, as you have to compete for the different tiles, and that's the best element.   There's a high amount of randomness in the shield drawing and the card drawing.   That seemed a bit awkward.   The theme is pretty much a "miss" and should not count towards any kind of Humanities credit.  Given the choice, I think I'd always choose Notre Dame over Louis.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Usually not a big fan of conventional area majority games, but this one is special. Perhaps I enjoyed the spatial linking mechanism.

Lots of micromanagement, but it was complex in an intuitive way that made sense and offered real variety instead of being more of the same. Dorn is the master of clean busy designs, if that makes sense.

RATING: 9
I played Louis XIV just twice so far and it was a 2 player game and a 4 player game.

I need to try Louis XIV a little more with 3 or 4 players to give Louis XIV a proper rating. I understood the a bit game more in the 4 player game.

Louis XIV is not an easy game to master. It takes a couple of game to get the hang of things.

The more I play, the better understanding I have. It`s becoming one of my favorites.

RATING: 9
An excellent new offering by Ruediger Dorn, an almost perfect game of considerable decision making trees. Where should I place my influence pawns? Should I try to fulfill missions or go for the insignia? Has for me the same potential as  "Puerto Rico" for continuous replay and "let's play again because I want to try something different" value. And it even takes aways very little physical space in your collection! But don't expect strong theme here, this is a very abstract game that creates little historic atmosphere...
The only slight problem is with the final additional VP's for insignia majority, that seems to jar with the perfect rest of the game (as it is pure luck of the draw, with no way to influence it), but it is possible to leave that rule away and just treat the insignia as simple VP's.

RATING: 6
I put this one in the same category as the other classic Kramer and Knizia titles of the early 2000s. Very dry, and not very sexy compared to today's offerings (amazing how much things have changed in less than 10 years already), but a solid play. The theme is pretty much tacked on and you're pushing wooden bits around some intricate rules, but if you want to play a clever area control game, this is as good a choice as any.

Like El Grande, it's a very tactical exercise, and there's a bit of randomness that bothers a lot of players. For me, I didn't mind the random card draws or even the notorious endgame shield scoring. Reminds me a bit of Goa in that good play will put you in the position to win and then you need to get a little lucky.

Years ago this would've come off the shelf with some regularity but now the design feels surpassed. It does suffer from a heavy rules overhead, so best to play with people who already know how to play it, otherwise there are better games for 4P that take a while to teach.

Suggested variant from Xssassin:

We use the suggested rule where your initial hand of missions is 1/1/1, maximum of 3, rather than 1/1/0, maximum of 2, and for the life of me I cannot figure out why this wouldn't just be the default. Are you supposed buy your medium, then draw a hard and hope you can play it with one wild and another leftover hex? C'mon. Also this reduces the odds of the dreaded "every mission in my hand shows the same symbol" mishap.

RATING: 8
[Resource Management, Area Majority] [4P:8, 3P:7] Hey, not bad for what is, on the suface, an area control game. Area control is just a median mechanism. The heart of the game is a cute, sort-of-complicated-and-clunky resource system which has the player trying to navigate an equation of converting time (represented by influence cards) into victory points (represented by coats-of-arms and mission cards). Everyone starts with four influence cards to play, then tries to parley those cards into more cards and resources via the "board" abilities and mission card advantages. It feels a lot like Goa, interestingly, replacing the auctions with the area majority engine. The aim is the same - generate more actions and resources by investing wisely - sort of like the Extra Actions and Development Tracks in Goa. Yes, there is quite a bit of randomness/luck here, what with four different card draws (money, influence, intrigue, and especially mission) and the coat of arms chit pulls. A lot's been made about the coat of arms chits, but I suspect the mission card draws influence the game far more than the couple of VPs that lucky majorities in coats of arms will give you. I don't mind the randomess because the resource relationships are so interesting. Overall, it's a nice little game in a nice little box. *** UPDATE: OK, so we underestimated this game. It's by far the most difficult Eurogame we have ever played. The timing and rhythm of the game gives me fits, and the confrontation within the game is very bloody. Ouch. You're not getting anything elegant here; it's a bunch of clockwork parts that you have to try to get moving with precision, or else you're really going to get screwed. There's only one way to win in this game, and it's to win brutally and ugly. Fits the game theme of politicking and intrigue to a T. I can't think of any other game I've played that's anywhere near the character of Louis XIV. This is a heavier-than-mediumweight game in the class of 5P Puerto Rico (okay, it's heavier than that), with about as much elegance (which is saying not much). Good game, worth owning and playing, but I like Goa a lot more due to the premium I put on game elegance. *** UPDATE: Definitely gets better the more the players are familiar with the game and the abilities conferred by the tiles and the mission cards. Very strong effort from Dorn, certainly just as impressive as Goa.

(Old pre-acquisition commentary follows)
March 2005. Rudiger Dorn + alea + renaissance theme = add to game library. Have high hopes that the usual alea production values will improve the look of the game from the Essen 04 pix. "Mid-sized" box a slight concern - would have been more comfortable if it was a big box release, screw the extra $. But with this title now tag-teaming with the Knizia title "Palazzo" as a 1-2 opening punch, this new mid-sized game line looks like it'll get some serious push. *** UPDATE: With Im Schatten and Turmzau falling by the wayside, this is now my top want along with Palazzo. I hear area majority, but not 1st 2nd 3rd, plus I hear "luck of the draw", "strange scoring" and "lots and lots of options". Sounds good, sign me up. (UPDATE: Just acquired! Yeah!)

RATING: 7
Louis XIV is a clever area control game that seems a little unusual for the alea small-box series. It isn't really difficult to explain but the amount of rules required for such a small title can be intimidating at first. Once you've gotten past that it still is somewhat fiddly, but in a good way.

All of the favours that can be garnered at the royal court are useful in one way or another, and while the card draws provide a good deal of randomness it never feels game-breaking. There is enough to think about without being overwhelming, and a lot of competition on the field. A compact and compelling little package.

RATING: 7
So nice to finally play this game.  It feels convoluted at first, but is actually a very simple Euro point-fest.  Has lots of little things going on that definitely demand repeat plays and consideration. The theme is virtually abstract (though I have read there are connections between the represented Persons and their effects), but the game plays well regardless of theme. LXIV has lots of things in it that are signature of a Dorn design, and it all comes together nicely. Lots of luck in this one too, but it's manageable luck and I like that in a game. This is not a game that would get everyone excited, but I find it a real solid entry for gamers who enjoy a cleaver use of mechanics in a typical Euro-style game. Would never recommend this as a 2-player game though. It really needs a full complement of 4 players.

RATING: 6
Played with 2, 3 and 4 players.  It is a very different game at each number, and I'm not sure it works well with any less than 4. With three, if one person follows a "spread out" strategy during a round - it seems necessary for the other two players to cooperate to stop them from getting too much benefit. If one of them chooses not to, then the other must do so or they both lose out to the third player.  Sort of a game of "chicken".   A good game, on the heavy side.  It seems O.K. with 2, but we found that certain card combinations can create huge advantages for one player, and with only two people playing, it can be difficult to recover.  The jury is out on this one... UPDATE:  I played again with 4 players and have decided that it really needs 4 to rate a "7".  The reason I rate it no higher is because there is just too much posibility of a non-in contention player being "thumped" because his card-draw happens to match what someone else is trying to do.  It also has a bit of a runaway leader problem.   I would rate it much lower with 3 or less players.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I've got a lot of love for this one. It really shines with 4 players. This game can be very stressful at turns with grueling decisions that need to be made. The more avenues you have access to the less confidence you seem to have in picking one. As you play more, you get a better sense on how to best set yourself up for the next round, and the fact that it is a tight game with only 4 rounds leaves you with a great deal of long term strategizing capabilities. With the amount of different roads you can take, each of them manage to be impressively balanced, there certainly aren't any useless tiles.

A lot of people mention the coat of arms 'luck' factor that seems to be just thrown in there. I think though it's more a probability thing, if you go MAINLY for coats of arms you still have a fighting chance because of that little scoring rule. I'm sure Dorn knew what he was doing, and you can always ignore that small rule if you don't agree.

To me, it seems the greatest imbalance is in the mission card rewards. Some offer GREAT benefits where your opponents really feel it. Others of the same cost are hardly beneficial.

Playing this one though is just a blast. It's a brain burner, you've got to consider a lot of possibilities with each move. With each game you can pursue a different avenue in attempts to win, but win or lose, it's a real gem in terms of gameplay experience.

RATING: 8
Louis XIV is very fun with two players, using the following official Alea two-player variant to the standard two-player rules. (I found this varient in a forum thread and rephrased here for my convenience).

Setup: Use 6 round cards instead of 4. In addition to materials for the two real players (the start player and the other player), use influence markers for two neutral (dummy) players: 
- For the first (1st) dummy player, use 8 influence markers.
- For the second (2nd) dummy player, use 4 influence markers.

Influence phase: Place the dummy players' influence markers according the following rules:

1. Place two markers for the 1st dummy player on the tile with Louis X1V. 
2. Draw an influence card, and place one marker for the 2nd dummy player on the corresponding tile.
3. If you are the start player, play one influence card from your hand and place your markers as normal.
4. If you are the other player, play one influence card from your hand and place your makers as normal.
5. Draw an influence card, and place two markers for the 1st dummy player on the corresponding tile.
6. Draw an influence card, and place one marker for the 2nd dummy player on the corresponding tile.
7. Repeat steps 3 through 6 until you place all neutral markers.
8. When all dummy players' markers are placed, continue in turn order to play your influence cards from your hands and place your markers, discarding your last cards as normal.
Note: If you draw a wild influence card for a dummy player, discard it and redraw.

Scoring: A dummy player can win a board (and the board is flipped as usual) or tie for a board, but does not receive any rewards. If a dummy player wins board six, do not place an additional marker.

RATING: 8
I like Louis XIV quite a lot, despite some nagging reservations. Let’s get the reservations out of the way first. The game is perhaps a bit overwrought. Systems are layered upon systems, and almost all of it is governed by card draws. There are several consequences of this design style.

First, the game is hard to teach. The level of rules overhead is high. This is more of a nuisance than a serious flaw, with one exception. The rules for what happens in Louis’s district are so convoluted that I find myself having to look them up every time I play.

Secondly, the game is rife with randomness. In general, I don’t mind randomness. The most common criticism of Louis XIV is the luck inherent in the end-game shield bonuses. Does this bother me? No, not really. Without it, I think the endgame would be too calculable. What’s more bothersome is the way that players are repeatedly handcuffed by the cards they draw. You get pot luck, and then you just have to plan out the best course of action based on the fate of the cards. I don’t mind reactive games like this, but Louis XIV really rubs the lack of control in your face: random income and Louis placement, random influence cards, random intrigue cards, random mission cards, etc. The rules complexity of Louis XIV makes me think that it wants to be a strategic planning game. But it never quite gets there, staying on the level of tactics and reaction.

As a game of tactics and reaction, though, Louis XIV is a success. The game gives you your cards just in the nick of time, allowing you to cobble together a short-term plan of action to make the best of your situation. There are a lot of interesting nooks and crannies in the tactical decisionmaking, making for an engrossing game. At least for four players. The game stinks with three in my experience, for two reasons. The first problem is that the turn order is imbalanced, with one player getting to go last twice – which is huge in this game. The second problem, the real game-breaker, is that there is not nearly enough competition on the board with less than four players. The players’ pieces will be spread too thinly across the tiles, sucking the tension and drama from the game like I suck the marrow from my Osso Bucco.

RATING: 8.5
Nine plays, once with two players, once with three, otherwise with four. I really enjoy the game play of this one, as each new twist and turn is unique in its pressures and demands, and the start of each round has the delightful feeling of picking through a well-stocked tackle box and trying to guess which of the shiny whatnots and colorful dangly doo-dads is going to land the most fish. However, like a well-stocked tackle box, Louis XIV is also rather inelegant. Another analogue might be that of a fanciful Enlightenment-era clockwork toy: finely tuned and dazzling in its intricacy, yet excessive, cold and hard to explain. I'd also like to say that there is something of a missed opportunity here, because I feel like the theme is convincingly built into the game but the publisher(s) didn't work hard enough to bring it out into the open. Even just a little flavor text on the influence and mission cards would have made a colossal difference, not to mention a suitable explanation as to what the coats-of-arms really represent. I understand that it is most likely economics that kept this from being the big-box game it should have been (Louis XIV is nothing if not a hobbyist's game), but that doesn't change the fact that Alea needs to work harder at selling their themes.

RATING: 8
This is a surprisingly tactical game that really makes you think about your available options and weigh them up carefully. There's a nice balance between completing missions and securing victory points, and the mechanics for getting your markers back from a space if you lose, and losing them if you win, work very well to balance the play and keep all players honest. The main reason I don't give this a higher rating is the coat of arms scoring factor at the end: this is completely random, and could undo all of your hard work at the end of the game by handing victory to an opponent that you have in every other respect outplayed for the whole game. To be fair, though, this has not actually happened in any game I've played so far, so perhaps the potential for that to happen is not such a problem. One other minor criticism: the missions contain no "flavour text" to describe what you're actually trying to do. This is not massively detrimental, and does not affect gameplay at all; rather, it's a missed opportunity to better reflect the theme. I should also mention there's a Louis XIV figure available in toy stores that looks much better on the table than the token that comes with the game: well worth getting hold of one.

RATING: 7.5
This is a thinking game, as you try to gain mission tokens by gaining the favor (majority of influence tokens) of a member of the court.  Influence placement mechanic is quite interesting, and the game has many subtleties.  Good reply value!

There is a bit of luck with the influence cards you are dealt, but this is practically negated since you have the option of moving influence tokens to an adjacent character, not to mention that you always have an extra influence card (therefore you will be discarding the least valuable).

RATING: 8
As the third member of Dorn's "Tailing Tower" Trilogy (I really don't think that that's a thing, but it's the common mechanical element between Louis XIV, Goa, and Traders of Genoa), it's a phenomenal area majority game for four. I would even go to say that it might even be the best area majority game with 4 (El Grande being the best with 5 and San Marco being the best with 3). The risk taking/management in this is wrenching. Sure, there are safe bets when courting Louis' inner circle, but you don't win if you don't take chances (on your group, as well as attempting to fulfill missions sight unseen).

RATING: 6
[Initial Impression: 7] 
There are several options to track, which I like, but overall the game feels clunky and inelegant. Some mechanisms almost seem added just for the sake of having "more". For example, the Intrigue cards and being able to take an extra Influence card add options, but at the expense of decreasing predictability of placements. Some character rewards seem clearly better than others, especially close to the end-game.  For a 2-player game, use the Alea 2-player variant, which has 2 dummies and positions them before each set of player turns during the influence phase. This strengthens the dummies and provides more competition/tension.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
One of my top, top games of all time.

I don't normally enjoy area majority games, but this one is different.

It punishes overextension and greediness, but if you're too meek you stand a chance of losing out on all presented opportunities.

You need to tread lightly enough not to rock the boat, but not too lightly so as not to make any impact.  Much as you'd expect of a life of intrigue in the Sun King's court.

The game has been criticized for unbalanced mission cards and too much luck with family crests in the final scoring, but I strongly disagree with both those sentiments.

RATING: 10
Wow.  I love this game.  I am a big fan of Rudiger Dorn.  His games really resonate with me.  This is a fantastic area control game.  The theme is wonderful, with beautiful cards with pictures of the members of the court of Louis XIV.  I've been playing the official Alea two player variant and it plays very very well.  There are two phantom people in the game.  It's very easy to think of those cubes as additional influences on the various members of the court.  It makes perfect sense to me and it feels very comfortable.  Most importantly it creates tension in the game.  This, Reef Encounter, Goa, and Caylus are my favorite medium to heavy two-player board games.

RATING: N/A
規則:
*以螺旋方式放腳色版塊1~12(1在中間，向右下開始轉)，八張黃金卡洗勻後抽掉四張，陰謀卡洗勻放置在11-12角色邊邊，影響卡洗勻放在11下面，任務卡按牌背洗勻分堆
*每人拿淺藍，中藍任務卡各一張，五塊錢，16個影響力標記，照順位將幾個擺在中間供應區5-5-6-7 (2p : 5-6)
*遊戲總共進行四輪，每輪有四個階段
A 供給 開金錢卡，每個人拿收入>起始玩家根據卡放置路易王>拿完成的任務卡獎勵>抽五張影響力卡
B 影響力階段 每人輪流出影響力牌直到出到剩一張，之後起始玩家將所有影響力牌洗勻成為牌庫，出牌後有兩個選擇
1. 放至多三個影響力標記(joker兩個)，從該牌角色開始放，放完第一個可選擇第二三個要不要移到相鄰的，第二個放完可選第三個要不要放到相鄰的
2. 從中間供應區拿最多三個影響力標記(joker兩個)回自己供應區
C 算分 照順位從1結算到12，每個版塊獎勵可能有三種結算條件
1. 金錢 第一名不用花錢，其他人可以花錢買
2. 名次 只有第一名有
3. 指定影響力 只要超過就有賞
*計分時出陰謀卡，可從中央放一個影響力到該版塊，或是自己供應區放兩個到該版塊，該牌放到陰謀卡牌庫底
*金錢名次只要有人第一 就會翻面，指定影響力有人拿也會翻面
*金錢名次 第一的人 要把影響力標記放到中央供應區，其他狀況都是放回自己供應區
*有路易王的版塊 第一名多拿一個皇冠， 第二名可以拿原本第一名的獎賞，其他照舊，第一名平手的話，都不拿皇冠，照原本規則走
D 任務階段 每個玩家用獎勵完成對應任務，每完成一張可在三疊抽一張新任務卡
*此階段結束只能保留一個任務版塊，多的都要換成加分板塊
*以上四階段完成後 換起始玩家，進入下一輪
*遊戲結束 沒用到的影響力卡，陰謀卡，任務版塊，遊戲版塊上的影響力標記，三塊錢 都可以換一個加分板塊，接下來每門加分板塊最多的在拿一個蓋著的加分板塊(平手都拿)，最後每個加分板塊一分，完成的任務卡五分，平手比自己供應區的影響力標記
*兩人遊戲時除了起始影響力標記(5-6)，那8個中立影響力標記，路易王放兩個，在抽三張影響力卡各放兩個(Joker重抽)，之後進入正常的第二階段

RATING: 4
If played with the following House rules, my rating goes up to an 6

Before Turn 1 changes:
Reveal X+1 of each pale and medium blue Royal Decree cards where X is the number of players and then draft them in a first-to-last player, last-to-first player order. (1,2,3,4,4,3,2,1)

Supply Phase changes
Reveal X Influence cards at the start of each Supply phase and draft them in clockwise order, starting with the first player, where X is the number of players multiplied by 5

Evaluation Phase Changes:
Search the Intrigue deck and choose a card to add to your hand when resolving noble #12. (François-Michel Louvois)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Very abstract influence game nominally about court intrigue in the court of the Sun King. Players play cards to put influence tokens on a number of nobles. Rewards are given for control of these nobles, and the rewards are used to buy mission cards, which are the primary source of end-game victory points.

Louis XIV, while definitely a solid game is definitely better with more players. Even so, like Goa I feel it is overwrought and slightly repetitive. Rüdiger seems to like piling rules and mechanisms into his games. One type of cards? Two types? No no no, in L14 you get gold cards, influence cards, mission cards (in three subtypes), and intrigue cards, and don't even get me started on influence "barrels", mission chips, coats of arms, gold coins, 12 different nobles, and the Louis figure. So, lots of different types of "things" all interacting - just like in Goa - but like that game it feels like a little too much for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The game is divided into four phases, but the only one that really matters is the area control phase.  Here is where people earn the pieces that will be used to complete missions.  Completed missions provide advantages in later rounds as well as score more points than anything else in the game.  I think the missions are overpowered and which missions are drawn can give a lot of an advantage.  As such, there is a good deal of luck in drawing good missions, but I have always liked a little luck in my games.

RATING: 10
It isn't often that I initially rate a game '10,' but this one has it all... money, random card play, limited turn actions, influence, a unique scoring system, relatively quick play time and it's just plain fun.

Rüdiger Dorn has taken some tried and true mechanics, mixed well, and designed a near perfect game.  One of the 'must have' games of 2005 (if only to keep your alea collection complete), it has definitely lived up to all of the hype.

If Dorn keeps pumping out games like Louis XIV, Emerald, Traders of Genoa, Jambo and Goa... he will fast become my favourite game designer!

RATING: 9
New Comment as of 10/2009:

Louis XIV was one of the first modern games I discovered. I used to be quite enchanted with it. The level of polish and intricacy here are both quite impressive. You have a limited amount of influence to wield at court (couriers, bribes, let your imagination run wild - or read Dangerous Liasons). All of the favors are worth winning, but the key is to stay out of escalating contests with other players, so that you win more favors more often. 

There are lots of little facets here. The basic premise is that you need the most influence with a given courtier to win (area majority), but sometimes it's winner takes all, sometimes you have the option to bribe even if you're in last, and sometimes it's a free-for all with a qualifying cut-off (awarded to everyone who places two influence, for example). These variations represent the changing mood at court, and add a small but appreciable dimension to the game's replayability. Each game you will build up unique advantages over your teammates based on your successes at court. Some say the key to the game is going last (which is a variable player power available in a few different guises), but I don't think it's a completely overpowering strategy. 

Anyway, "intricate" is a good word for this game. Not heavy, not super complex, but intricate. I've played 2 hour games of this and hope to never repeat the experience. I used to think this was a reliably 90 minute game. Perhaps with new gamers. If you have experience with games, you will recognize the various elements that are blended here, and this can make for a snappy hour of game play.

I think this is acceptable mainly because it is different from a lot of the scheming strategy games that I tend to enjoy, where you can take very direct action against a particular player (and where you can also predict the possibility of actions against yourself by specific others).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is an very good game that I think may be suffering a bit from mistaken expectations.  The smaller box size and moderate price may be leading people to think that this is a "gamer's" middleweight a la Ra or Saint Petersburg.

It's not.

Louis XIVth is a heavyweight game, in the same weight class as Torres, Puerto Rico or Princes of Florence.  There are numerous systems at work together here, producing tough decisions, heavy gameplay, and a significant learning curve.  In that context, the length of the rulebook becomes more acceptable to me, although the rules still feel a tad fiddlier than other games of its weight.  Learning the characteristics of the different person tiles is roughly analogous to learning the buildings in Puerto Rico, but the sequence of play is among the harder to grasp for modern Euros (it's not as complex as wargames like Hammer of the Scots or Crusader Rex, though).

The complexity doesn't bother me though.  The game play is rich, with measures of strategy, tactics, bluff and misdirection.  The bang for the complexity isn't as high as it is for the best Euros, but it is still quite high.

All of the bitching about the endgame shield-majority bonuses baffles me.  Yes, it is semi-random, but more important than the randomness is the fact that it helps to balance shield play against mission play, offering a few points compensation for the loss of special powers granted by completed missions.

A bigger issue for me is the Intrigue cards.  They play a pretty marginal role in the game, since they are only available via one personality tile, and a couple of missions.  I would be surprised if more than 4-5 appear in a given game.  Also, the "surprise influence" that they grant isn't as interesting as I expect from something that is so rare and exotic.  Perhaps they would be more fun if they offered one-time use special powers, although they would then have to be balanced carefully against each other. 

For such a complex game, player aids would have been a really nice addition to the package, though.  In fact there are several signs of production decisions made to fit the game into a particular price point rather than for quality, including smooth finishes for all cards and tiles rather than linen finish, cards that could have benefited from a move up to the next standard size, and thinner than expected (although still adequate) chits and tiles.  You get your money's worth, but a higher level of production (and the accompanying rise in price) would have been nice for a game of this weight.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
A very fiddly game (which is why a number of BGG members have made their own boards to manage all the pieces).

First few games can be challenging, but it seems that this quickly becomes addictive.

One reviewer said: "I’ve never played Wallenstein, but I see very little in common between Louis and Puerto Rico. If I were to compare Louis XIV with other games I would say it's like a mixture of El Grande and Wyatt Earp. Another game that has a similar feel is Mykerinos in which you are trying to get area majorities in order to get special powers and points. Mykerinos, however, is much simpler and lighter." Another review (http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/652283/is-there-a-louis-xiv-lite-somewhere) compared it to Mykerinos, Blue Moon City, and Leonardo da Vinci (all games that we own but have yet to get to the table; therefore, consider playing all those before buying another game). Another commenter in the same forum suggested Palais Royale (http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/38992/royal-palace).

Amazon reviewer said: "Not really a great game for kids of boardgaming novices, because it is moderately complex. However, this is a reasonably good game for an experienced gamer."

Although it can be played with 2, it's weight is 3.0 and it plays in 100 minutes!

Some negative criticism as a 2 player game and one that did not go over well with the wife: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/559828/louis-xiv-when-good-themes-go-bad

Amazon review said: Recommended for experienced gamers. Although the game's mechanics are fairly simple in practice, the game sounds very complex when the rules are explained and will likely intimidate players not familiar with European style games.

RATING: 6
[Played 6 times] 
Tactical game or risk management, where imperfect information prevents too much "analysis-paralysis"; it has some luck involved, several choices available and a moderate playing time. 
It adds a twist on the classical area-majority system. 
I strongly dislike the rule assigning extra points when game ends in a totally random way.
The 2-player game is very dependent on luck once both players have some experience, as the real difference is made by the occasional lucky draw of the "Influence Cards" and/or by the system awarding extra VPs for blazons when game ends. I'm afraid this is the case with more players as well, but it's more difficult to notice at least!

RATING: 8
"Wooden cubes majority" game with complex scoring and a good measure of resource management (money, two kinds of influence tokens, mission tokens etc.) tossed in. An intense middle-weight game in a new box format for Alea. I like this game very much. The theme, being indeed very Knizia-like (i.e. chromeless), is not that thin at all. Just look at the arrangement of the characters and their "payouts" and compare these to the roles they actually played at Louis' court. This game could turn out to be the ultimate majority game (in the same sense as Ra sometimes is considered the ultimate auction game). But that only repeated play will tell.

RATING: 7
The main focus of the game is an interesting worker placement/bidding mechanic that is different than any other game.  The worker management aspect is also intriguing.  There is a tradeoff because when you win a free benefit you lose your workers.  At times you may wish to have a minority of workers on a tile so that you can buy the benefit and get your workers back.  And yet, if you always do this, you will run out of money.  So, there is a balance to be had in doing this while at the same time working to block your opponents.

RATING: 7
This is a tense and interesting game with tough choices, nail-biting moments and a really great theme enhanced by (generally) nice components. Use your influence at court to gain favours which you can use to complete missions or buy marks of heraldry. The king visits different royals (for which there is an important spatial aspect) and gives bonuses. The results of the reward round change by flipping the various personages when their benefits are claimed. 

This is a clever game where the influence phase plays similarly to the Market phase in Hermagor (which is by far the most interesting bit of that game.) Louis XIV goes further, however - I love the way the successful bidder is penalised by having his courtiers sent back to the country (general stock). I mark it down, however, for its slightly annoying card mechanic that determines where you can start playing influence. This isn't too much of a problem. 

I do not, however, think much of the random shield grab when you win heraldry tokens, nor the silly bonus tokens for those who've randomly picked up the most of one heraldic emblem over another. Eclipse re-implements this in a more elegant form with the tile draws for combat etc. (though it's still annoying in that game). But for the end game randomness, this would be a point higher.

Maybe I should try Five Tribes as I like the workers-on-a-path mechanism so much.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Interesting second cousin to El Grande (a mutated cousin, that is).

RATING: 8
Played twice with three people.  This is a very complex area influence game that took a good bit of explaining and a few turns of play to pick up (the game comes with no player cheat sheets; they would be insanely helpful).  The heart of the game is placing influence markers on various locations on the board; the person with the most influence markers on any given location gets some reward (which will help them fulfill missions, get victory shields, or get money); however, being first is not always good, as the person with the most markers on a location loses all those influence markers.  There appear to be many possible paths to victory; the high player interaction (competing for locations on the board), very limited resources, and limited number of turns makes the game very thought-heavy.  Very, very fun, but not a game for novice gamers.

RATING: 4
After another annoying game of this last night, I find myself still thinking about it today. There's more strategy than I initially thought, as you really have to consider the flipping of tiles from round to round; anyone who tries too hard in the first round will get burned in the second. Once someone gets a leg up on the missions, the game is essentially over, as they not only have the lead but also additional benefits that help them extend that lead. My girlfriend enjoys it, so I guess I'll be forced to play it more. Hopefully something more compelling than "don't win too much in the first round" will emerge as a strategy.

UPDATE: After not playing this for over two years, I was able to get rid of it. I guess she didn't like it so much after all!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
"Order out of Chaos" . . . should be the other sub-title of this game.  After reading the rules, I was still scratching my head as to what I was actually doing or respresenting in this very abstract game of political intrigue.  Finally, after playing a few games, I have a much better understanding AND enjoyment of the game.  What appears on its face as a some-what chaotic (the "board" or grouping of cards, the varied places for the pieces, the seemingly abstract missions, etc.) game is really an area control via networking game.  Once you understand this (and the basic four phases of the game) it plays rather quickly and can lead to some agonizing decisions during its course.  The combination of theme and play is not as tight as Puerto Rico, but then again it is not as abstract of China or Web of Power (although its is definately more "fiddly" than those two . . . whatever that means).  My only advice is that you don't make up your mind based on only a couple of plays--it takes a few plays to get the feel and fun of this game.  Awards: Meeples' Choice 2005, Deutscher Spiele Preis 2005 (Germany).

RATING: 8
In the court of Louis the XIV all manner of individuals are continually jockeying for influence and favour with the many nobles that reside there. 

Offering an area control mechanism of sorts, each noble offers a different benefit. The the aim is to earn the most victory points by completing missions and collecting heralds. Louis is a classic Euro game that plays in just over 1 hour. For the depth of play and time frame that it delivers, Louis is in  my top 5 Euro list.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I don't think I've ever played a game where I've had to refer to the rules as often as Louis XIV. What does that tile give you again? When is it flipped? What does the second player get? What does the tied player get? Where do the influence markers go afterwards? How does the presence of Louis XIV change all this? Never has a player aid been more warranted than here!

That said, Louis XIV does offer plenty of literal intrigues. How should I use my cards to optimally spread my influence? Which favors are most desirable for my short term and long term objectives? What are the other players up to and how should I best cross/avoid them? Yes, there is a randomness from the card drawing but it's you who decides how best to play your cards given the circumstances at the court. The theme of court intrigues at the Sun King's court is not far fetched at all.

RATING: 7.5
Area influence game based on 17th century France.  There are 12 figures represented by 12 boards. Move your pawns onto these boards to achieve majorities or have a presence to earn tokens. Tokens allow you to fulfill missions, worth 5 VPs each. Other boards let you get accumulate other chips, worth 1 VP each. It's been an engaging game with 2,3 or 4 players. Plan to spend two hours playing this game.

RATING: 6.5
I think this game is pretty good, but I wouldn't buy this for myself. It's not as easy to teach as it should be. I've played this game with multiple people, and every one of them has to look back at the rules to see how ties are resolved.

This should not happen for this game. In El Grande, for example, it is very simple to explain what happens on ties: no one gets the points for that place; the tied players get the points for the next scoring position down. Louis XIV should have been as simple to explain.

RATING: 7.25
規則:
*以螺旋方式放腳色版塊1~12(1在中間，向右下開始轉)，八張黃金卡洗勻後抽掉四張，陰謀卡洗勻放置在11-12角色邊邊，影響卡洗勻放在11下面，任務卡按牌背洗勻分堆
*每人拿淺藍，中藍任務卡各一張，五塊錢，16個影響力標記，照順位將幾個擺在中間供應區5-5-6-7 (2p : 5-6)
*遊戲總共進行四輪，每輪有四個階段
A 供給 開金錢卡，每個人拿收入>起始玩家根據卡放置路易王>拿完成的任務卡獎勵>抽五張影響力卡
B 影響力階段 每人輪流出影響力牌直到出到剩一張，之後起始玩家將所有影響力牌洗勻成為牌庫，出牌後有兩個選擇
1. 放至多三個影響力標記(joker兩個)，從該牌角色開始放，放完第一個可選擇第二三個要不要移到相鄰的，第二個放完可選第三個要不要放到相鄰的
2. 從中間供應區拿最多三個影響力標記(joker兩個)回自己供應區
C 算分 照順位從1結算到12，每個版塊獎勵可能有三種結算條件
1. 金錢 第一名不用花錢，其他人可以花錢買
2. 名次 只有第一名有
3. 指定影響力 只要超過就有賞
*計分時出陰謀卡，可從中央放一個影響力到該版塊，或是自己供應區放兩個到該版塊，該牌放到陰謀卡牌庫底
*金錢名次只要有人第一 就會翻面，指定影響力有人拿也會翻面
*金錢名次 第一的人 要把影響力標記放到中央供應區，其他狀況都是放回自己供應區
*有路易王的版塊 第一名多拿一個皇冠， 第二名可以拿原本第一名的獎賞，其他照舊，第一名平手的話，都不拿皇冠，照原本規則走
D 任務階段 每個玩家用獎勵完成對應任務，每完成一張可在三疊抽一張新任務卡
*此階段結束只能保留一個任務版塊，多的都要換成加分板塊
*以上四階段完成後 換起始玩家，進入下一輪
*遊戲結束 沒用到的影響力卡，陰謀卡，任務版塊，遊戲版塊上的影響力標記，三塊錢 都可以換一個加分板塊，接下來每門加分板塊最多的在拿一個蓋著的加分板塊(平手都拿)，最後每個加分板塊一分，完成的任務卡五分，平手比自己供應區的影響力標記
*兩人遊戲時除了起始影響力標記(5-6)，那8個中立影響力標記，路易王放兩個，在抽三張影響力卡各放兩個(Joker重抽)，之後進入正常的第二階段

RATING: 8.5
A great game with fantastic presentation and very good mechanics! My top game in majority area control! It's also great to introduce newbies into more complex gameplays. The historic setting is simply genius and actually manages coherently combine the theme and the gameplay! Simply fantastic stuff!

Played this game again after a very long time and I still love it. I feel actually great affection to it. I think this game is the one that made me fall in love with board games. I had only a very small "collection" of maybe two or three games by then, and as I got this I was showered by a feeling of magic! Opening this beautiful little box and discovering all the bits and pieces! The originality of not having any board to play on but only these absolutely gorgeous character tiles!

You can tell that a lot of love went into this one! I understand the importance of Goa in terms of a gamers game statement, but to me, this is his opus magna, everything from concept to design is simply breathtaking, and the game still manages to create tension among players!

I LOVE IT!

[b]- Defying the test of time! 5+ years and I still can't give it a pass![/b]

RATING: 7
Un gioiellino. Il campo di battaglia è una corte del Re, per cui non troverai morti ammazzati. L'orticello però è troppo piccolo e i coltelli, seppur metaforici, volano lo stesso. Questo è Louis XIV: un gestionale dove più che fare il tuo devi rompere uova negli altrui panieri. Come gli scacchi, le regole sono semplici, le strategie complessissime. Mi piace la meccanica di gioco d-i-a-b-o-l-i-c-a, l'interazione pesante tra i giocatori che ne consegue e quei ribaltamenti di fronte che, ahimé, ho sperimentato sulla pelle nella partita inaugurale. Tutto serve nella stessa misura. Anche i soldi fanno brodo nella conquista della vittoria. Unica perplessità: il fattore-culo non è indifferente, sia nella scelta delle carte influenza che nei benefit delle missioni concluse. Poi, una considerazione di meta-gioco. C'è un aspetto di Louis XIV (ma forse sarebbe più corretto dire "c'è un atteggiamento nei riguardi dei giocatori") che mi ha particolarmente colpito: le regole dicono che è possibile chiedere favori ai personaggi adiacenti a quelli che hai in mano. Un Reiner Knizia si sarebbe fermato qui: le regole stabiliscono questo, punto. L'autore invece giustifica questa possibilità: le "adiacenze" (e la possibilità di sfruttarle) ci sono perché rappresentano la rete di relazioni che i membri della corte intessevano tra di loro (mi piace pensare che ci sia stata anche una ricerca storica in tal senso). E io, che prediligo il non astratto e che temo gli astratti mascherati da giochi a tema, non posso che apprezzare lo sforzo nel mettere il sistema di regole a servizio dell'ambientazione.

RATING: 8
Excellent! Tense and plays quickly. The limit of 4 turns make this game a little more strategic than it already is. The only luck part is in the draw of the influence cards. That can really affect whatever plans a player may have but it also makes a player think carefully on how they would play their cards to get the maximum effect. Another thing that I like about this game is how one player's decision directly affects another in terms of placing influence counters. Sometimes I am forced to change my strategy in order to combat the ones placed by another player. I would have to consider if wasting so much influence for a counter is worth it as any player who wins a chamber loses their influence counters to the general store. The mapboard is somewhat refreshing as well, making all its pieces squeezable into a small and compact box. Excellent game IMO.

A fairly complex game to learn from the rulebook but with a light feel to it. I don't know why, but grasped games like Puerto Rico, Power Grid and Imperial much faster. My wife picked up this quickly though, thinking it was much easier than Notre Dame. 

But I like it, very much. It's a quiet, pleasant and elegant game with lots of replay value and with the possibility to do some nasty tactical moves. My only complaint is that it has a distant feel to it. The game have really nice components and all, but when playing I feel I'm doing something arbitary rather than trying to influence people in the court of the king. But I like it just the same.

I play 2-player games with the variant with two virtual players (8/4 wooden pieces).

RATING: 8
What a pleasant suprise! All those fiddly bits, little rules and overall randomness, and yet Louis XIV is a deep, strategic game. There's a ton of interaction and chaos, but it's manageable. 

Managing is what you're doing a lot. You manage cards, cubes, money and tokens, but also risk, competition, position and options. Do I wait, push forward, fight, bluff, invest, spread out? It's your choice how much risk you want to take, how much you want to fight your opponents, how much you want to work towards future turns.

When playing Louis XIV, you have to work with the resources you randomly get and you must react to what the other players do. Much chaos, little control. On the other hand, the game rewards planning, and you can do a lot to increase your future options.

Little control, lots of decisions. :thumbsup:
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
-

This is a strategy game by Rudiger Dorn (Goa, Traders of Genoa, Jambo). It is fundamentally an area influence/control game, although the `areas' are actually the nobility figures surrounding the Sun King. A game of great design with some original elements and highly engaging game play. For me the primary negative is the Intrigue cards. They really hurt this nice game by introducing a random "take-that" element. Minus one star for the Intrigue cards (from 8 to 7).

RATING: 9
The horrendous rulebook delayed my enjoyment of this game for some time. Then someone taught the game to me. The rulebook really should be scrapped and rewritten. The game isn't that convoluted at all. I first thought 'good game'; however, upon subsequent plays this area majority game reveals its true genius. Rating has jumped from 8 to 9 after 4 plays and will probably stay there for quite a long time. Positives: Great design, interesting presentation, very tense throughout. Negatives: Possible luck issue depending on cards drawn, otherwise nothing.

RATING: 5
This is a perfectly fine game, where on your turn you drop 1-3 markers in a mancala sort of path along a series of 1-3 tiles. The card you play dictates where in the grid you can start your path from. Once everyone is done playing cards, the tiles are evalutates for mosties. The person with the most gets a discount, or maybe does the thing alone, and then if possible, other players pay for the same effect. The goal of the game is points, which come from picking up flag tokens and completing quest cards, which all want certain things, and all have neat powers that give you a vague sense that you are building an engine. Again, the game is fine, it's just that the little engine you create isn't that satisfying, and there is a lot of chaos, so going early in play order is a pretty large disadvantage. This is by no means game breaking as turn order passes each of the 4 rounds, but it does cause a certain lack of caring. This is a competently designed game that's just not that much fun. 

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:nostar: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:nostar: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment

RATING: 8
Every once in a while it's good to play an old classic that you've never played before. Sometimes, it's disappointing. I was a little disappointed by Caylus on my first play because I felt like other games did the worker placement better. Perhaps if I'd played Caylus before playing those other games, I'd have fallen in love with it. But with Louis XIV, I didn't feel any disappointment. It felt fresh and unique, and the card mechanic meshed really well with the majority control mechanic, and the way the tiles flip for variety was also very neat. Louis XIV is a tense, exciting game with tons of player interaction.  It's heavy without inducing migraines, and the interaction introduces a sense of lightness as does the randomness of the card play.

Louis XIV plays like Royal Palace wants to play.  There's always something to think about and there is good reason to be engaged while the other players take their turns.  What each player does will probably affect you.  The only other euros I've played with this level of interaction have had auction mechanics.
