RATING: 5
Based on one play.  It's clever, but I can't say I enjoyed it that much. A lot of it has to do with the number of rules (quite a lot for a 45-min game) and the special ability cards (I rarely like special abilities--it's too difficult to remember who can do what, and how to react to to other people). 

My biggest complaint, however, is that it is impossible to predict the actions of your fellow players.  The 'goals' of each player are hidden, and almost no information is available to help you glean this information (until it's too late anyways).  So strike one for being a game where you can't sense, even a little bit, what your opponent wants at times when it matters.  Second, your opponents' choices of moves depend on the special abilities that they have (too many to keep track of) and the courtier cards in their hands (very luck driven and impossible to determine at all based on prior actions of the player). So strike two for not having any sense of each player's future movement capabilities.  Therefore, you sort of play solitaire, without consideration of other people's future moves. Yes there's interaction between people, in a sense, but nothing more meaningful than rubbing up against strangers in a crowded subway.  Not my sort of game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
An extraordinarily elegant game, with interesting decisions all over the place.  Playing feels like balancing half a dozen see-saws at once, but the play itself is smooth and sensible.  There are tactics, strategy and psychology all in abundance, and though there is more luck than you might expect in a game like this, it doesn't bother me nearly as much here as it does in, say, T&E or Settlers.  And to top it off, the game (for me) just plain looks nice.

RATING: 9
Great Area Influence Game. Each round, players have a hand of 5 cards, each depicting 1 of the "Boards". Influence Markers can be placed only on these cards (you play 1 card per turn and put 3 markers on that card). You can then move your markers, but must always leave one behind. All 5 cards aren't used though, you only use 4 and discard the last one. Each "Board" Gives you a Bonus, like a certain counter, or card, or money. The basis of VP's is your trying to complete Mission cards that say "X Counter and X Counter" where X is one of the Counters that 1 of the "Boards" is offering. Also, Mission cards have a special text power that give you some sort of bonus when you have completed it. A very diverse Game that is tense and fun. Also, I love the Theme and Components.

RATING: 7.5
Louis XIV -- Played four-player.  In most regards, the rulebook is well done -- concise where possible, examples where necessary (illustrated and explained).  However, certain key elements which should be highlighted are hidden amongst unbolded text (e.g.: the fact that crown tiles are wild is stated clearly, but it's buried amongst a surfeit of other details.).  There are many (many!) such details to learn, requiring a very thorough reading of the rulebook.  That said, once you know the rules they're intuitive and easy to remember.  

And worth it.  The game system is innovative.  The decisions are tough.  Will your Influence cards allow you to reach the personage you want with enough tokens to get a reward?  Should you try to get the specific chips on your mission cards?  Or crown chips?  A mix?  

I find myself wondering if mission cards aren't worth a bit too much.  My only real complaint is that the two cards which let you draw additional mission cards are in the light stack, when their value clearly warrants the dark stack.  An even more minor issue:  I wish the coat-of-arms tiles were shaped like shields rather than squares, but the overall aesthetic is consistent and pleasing.

I've created a board to facilitate the placement of the placards and the associated tokens. (http://www.boardgamegeek.com/image/251729)

RATING: 7
Has many good qualities, and is an enjoyable game. Unlike Goa, the fine balance is not totally wrecked by the wild swings of the tacked-on luck elements, although the tacked-on luck elements do seem superfluous. Some fixes on this site have already address this issue.

The game lacks elegance. Missions are 5 points each, shields are 1 point each, and nothing else matters. Too many cards are unbalanced, and too many things don't matter in the first or last round. All the leftovers are traded for shields at the end of the game, which just feels like poor design work.

Plays well with two players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First of all, there ain't no intrigue going on here.  This is basically an abstract area control game with some kooky rule twists, special powers and pretty portraits.    That said, it is soundly solid for what it is.  The game offers many tough decisions and keeps you gripped 'till the end. Then of course, that is where the problems come in.   

There is a nice amount of luck that manipulates how you play rather than controlling it.  I like the use of Dorn's trademarked movement here, I'm always impressed how he finds ways to manipulate the same movement system into something that feels surprisingly new. 

But the shield scoring at the end is horrid and should be eliminated entirely, there is no reason to sweat over a couple points throughout the game, only to be randomly granted points at the end.  The secret is to luck into bonus points and not sweat the small stuff.  

This shield issue is minor to the biggest problem I have with the game. It seems that the card draw gets way too lucky.  Draw an objective, luck into big points draw again.  I won a game based solely on a run of really lucky draws.  I was randomly getting helmets and whatnot and just seeing what I could draw up.  A very hollow victory.

Then, of course, the cards are completely imbalanced but, I do have to say that the powers are quite fun and the cards to fulfill when they work as planned keep interests high, but for as much as this game has, I didn't walk away loving it, merely liking it, perhaps that will change with more plays.  

Update: After my last play, I've had zero desire to play this again.  I've ended up turning the game down multiple times.  It lost its luster quick.

Update: Hmm, played it again a years later, and while I still see the same problems in it, I still really enjoyed it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
LXIV is an area majority game, in which winning an area confers special rewards or abilities.  The regions are small square cards representing members of the Sun King's court.  Some of the cards give victory points, others provide "power chits", which bear symbols of helm, ring, scroll, holy hand grenade, or crown (wild), others allow movement or placement of additional influence markers.  On your turn, you play a card from your hand that allows placement of up to 3 influence markers on a specific region.  Once placed, some of the influence markers can be walked to diagonally adjacent cards.  Completing missions is what garners the most victory points.  Each player has two mission cards in every round (there are 4 rounds).  To fulfill a mission, a player must have the necessary power chits in hand (won in the area majority part of the game).

The components are just beautiful.  Gameplay is solid, with lots of tension.  There's plenty of scrambling for influence over the regions that provide power chits, and some bluffing.  Players are forced to vie for the same benefits, but the missions provide players with slightly different goals.  There are multiple layers of randomness to the game, but I don't feel it detracts.  I don't see this moving up to a 9 for me, but I do want to play it a bunch of times to find out!

RATING: 6
when our group first tried to play, the game was completely impenetrable. we quit rather than continue with this strange burden. thankfully, the game owner made a study of the rules and led us through an enjoyable second game.  

while the core is bidding, this is modulated by other mechanisms and a rewarding variety of choices. despite the renaissance portraits, it is very abstract and any attempts to marry theme to mechanisms should be ignored. after all, is louis walking around stepping on a bunch of paintings?

there are a lot of unnecessary complications. set-up is fiddly diddly and the scoring of the shields takes an unexpected left turn. not for casual players.

RATING: 7
Generic Eurogame where you need to place varying amounts of influence markers to gain favour with some of the courtesans of the king (who make up the playing board). They in turn reward you with various items that you need to exchange for mission cards (victory points). It's playable enough but a few issues let it down, which means that it lags behind the plethora of games of this ilk a bit. I feel as though there's maybe one or two elements of strategy missing from the gameplay. These in turn would have increased the amount of decisions you have to make and would certainly have enhanced the playing experience. I also feel as though currency is too easy to come by in the game, again reducing the amount of decisions you have to make (it feels like a very easy game to play in that sense. You can just buy your way out of trouble in some situations). It might have been improved if players didn't receive the 5 gold at the start or if only the starting player received the gold bonus at the start of each round. Despite these flaws it's still an enjoyable enough game to play.

RATING: 6
Played two player, rating may rise (or may not!). 'Okay' would be my verdict. Some feel of courtly intrigue, but not a lot. Decision making was lightish, though doubtless this would improve with more players (though I guess control would decrease and frustration increase). Essentially a mix of El Grande, Goa and one of Richard Breese's resource management games. It lacks the hard edge of any of those because of the hidden goals for players which is a questionable design choice. 

Enjoyable, but more interesting because of the ideas the game puts in one's head.

PS Having gone through the rules once, and had a playthrough, it was much easier to understand this game than the fiddly rules would suggest.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Gameplay-wise, this is about a 7. It's a light area control game where you play a card to determine where you can place your pieces onto the board. You have to balance your money, your supply of markers, and your tokens. Simple to set up and plays pretty quickly. Not a bad 3 or 4 player game.

My rating is lowered because of the insane end-game scoring that awards points completely at random. The game has that "classic" euro feel where each player is usually tied or within one or two points of the other players, so the random end-game bonuses mean that the winner is usually just determined at random. I don't see how that got past playtesting. If I play again, we'll be definitely using a variant to fix the scoring problem.

Overall, a light, enjoyable game that is hampered by one major flaw.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
 Trying to get the most rewards without losing your strength for the next round.    The game is quite intuitive.   It feels like an auction game. 2 players has limited shelf life.  Its good for about 5 plays, but the players can learn to optimize the sparse board and it comes down to luck of the dummy player(s) and the coat of arms. With 3 players, the board is too sparse again plus it adds a fiddly rule. Its a fun efficiency game with 4 players, but ultimately its the only player count the game holds up to repeated play.  Its essentially a fun next step after gateway game for 4 players only.  Incredibly niche to say the least.  I still enjoy it, but the situations in which this game shine are few and far between.

Tactical, yet allows for different strategic styles.  

As far as the shields, they score, you know they'll score, make sure you get your points for their scoring. 

Many plays in and revisited.  The game dropped the ball.  More difficult missions should have been included.  I understand the balance reasons, but the common power interactions become played out and predictable after 20 plays.  A new expansion with more missions, and a possible ruleset for drafting missions to synergize would do wonders, however I don't think it will happen.  Newer games, which create controlled combos and many more interactions have outpaced this game leaving it mostly obsolete.  An expansion could fix this, but I can't see one coming down the pipeline.
 
The 2 player game evolves into a luck fest as the max strategy ceiling is intuitive and easily hashed out, the 3 player game has too much space, but with 4 its still a fun game.  I am retired from the 2 and 3 player game, so my personal scale for this game is from 4-4.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I knew I would like this game when I saw who else was cheering for it and that it had Rudiger Dorn's name on it, and I was not disappointed. It is an area-influence game, but (unlike El Grande for example) the 'board' resets every time things are evaluated, so a leader is easier to beat up on. There is some possibility of a runaway leader with the Mission cards; if one player gets too big a power advantage he can become hard to stop. In fact that happened in my first game as I ran away with it, but my analysis is that that is not likely to happen with a little bit of experience. And in fact in my third game, just a little bit of focus on a leader (even though I wasn't leading as strongly) and a little bit of emphasis on how valuable the crowns are led to a much tighter game, which I narrowly lost. I do not think there is a runaway leader problem. The powers of the mission cards allow an early leader to mitigate some of the beating that he will get from experienced players, but they are not so overwhelming that it is not possible to overcome. 

My second play was with 3 players. We missed the rule about the guy who starts twice getting an intrigue card as compensation. Nevertheless, I do not think the 3-player game is as good as the 4-player version. The bidding wars are just not tight enough, and it is too easy to win tiles cheaply.

RATING: 5
The game this wanted to be is good, but the game that it IS falls short of expectations.  The influence is a simple and effective system, but I don't necessarily love the flipping of tiles.  It adds variance to the rounds, but there's so few of them, and the rounds where the major tiles are all first-player only are so vicious, that it can mean major screwage for players.  Some of the cards are much, much better than others, even within the same difficulty level, and there's no compensation for drawing a stinker.  In many ways, this has made all the sacrifices of theme and flavor that define Euros, but doesn't have strong enough mechanics to keep it interesting.

RATING: N/A
Players alternate placing guys on the board, then collect tokens from the various spots. Some spots require a majority, some a set amount of guys, some cost gold. Randomly dealt cards determine where you can place guys, so you can't always get the guys you need in the spots you want. Players spend their collected items on mission cards, which provide abilities, as well as points for end game. Repeat for several rounds, and you're done.

Not spectacular, but it's quick and fun. A solid 7 so far.

RATING: 7
Louis is in my view the most interesting implementation of Dorn's moving tower concept, and for that mechanic alone is worth having in my collection, as the experience is both highly entertaining and unique. Ultimately it is a tactical affair- you draw cards of various types and see what you can do with them. But there are enough bells and whistles here to keep the decisions engaging, plus the tension of when, where and how to commit your influence is delicious. Solid mid-weight game, albeit with a heavy rules overhead.

RATING: 7
A unique and good area control game. The only reason I do not rate it higher  because it is fiddly in a bad way. By this I mean there is lot of moving around of bits for the sole purpose of distributing points or clearing the board. You do not make any decisions and are just executing a mechanical chore. This extends the game's length to more than it should be and leaves me less than satisfied.

Contrast this to Puerto Rico, which also has a lot of moving parts but they seem well integrated in the game and thematially. So, I would call Puerto Rico fiddly in a good way 

Louis has mechanics that are very nice though and I do enjoy the core gameplay.
If it were more streamlined, I would rate it a 8.

RATING: 8
A very unconventional game, but an enjoyable one. I find the influence mechanics to be very interesting and unlike anything else I've played. I also like that there are multiple viable paths to victory. The two major black spots for me are that some missions are much more helpful than others (to the point where I almost want to house rule a Race for the Galaxy-like "start mission", except I think that would make the first round less interesting), and that the coat of arms scoring adds an unwelcome random element to that particular method of scoring (which has affected finishing order in about a quarter of my plays, and victory once). Overall, it's a fun game; I gave it to a friend of mine who likes it more than I do, but he's in my gaming group so I still get to visit :)

RATING: 7
This is a very interesting game.  The strategy is very subtle, but I think it is ultimately too luck of the draw driven to make it to my upper echelon of games.  

I enjoy the mission card system, but some missions are MUCH more powerful than others.  You can also get hosed by your missions requiring the same chits (helmets, rings, etc.) as your other missions.  This increases your dependency on winning crowns which is not a good thing in my mind.  

That said, it is a unique game in my group, and it will probably get some more play.  However, I doubt that it will become a mainstay in the group as many other games have.

RATING: 9
{ P - [3] [4] }

Solid area control game centered around efficiency and securing benefits for no more than they're worth. Creative board structure, good production values, innovative design in how the rooms are scored in sequence and some missions / cards / rooms can affect other rooms. The missions within difficulty tiers are unique but balanced, and when you have certain missions, the benefit from certain rooms can become more useful (e.g. winning ties mission + room 6, or three guys mission + room 11).  Money and bribing is a secure route that offers reward with minimal piece investment and reclaiming your influence; it's often tricky to determine if the best route is straight winning your mission tiles or money + bribing them. Coat of Arms offers a scoring mechanism that isn't as high risk as mission tiles.

Downside I think the hard double-helmet "super jester" mission isn't good enough (maybe throw down 5 pieces instead of 4) and the medium 2/2/2 missions are a bit too good.

We use the suggested rule where your initial hand of missions is 1/1/1, maximum of 3, rather than 1/1/0, maximum of 2, and for the life of me I cannot figure out why this wouldn't just be the default. Are you supposed buy your medium, then draw a hard and hope you can play it with one wild and another leftover hex? C'mon. Also this reduces the odds of the dreaded "every mission in my hand shows the same symbol" mishap.

RATING: 9.5
This is another game that I love. Yes, it's a bit fiddly, but the benefits massively outweigh this minor drawback. I also think that, for an abstract game, the theme really works well.

The one drawback to this game is the ridiculous game-end messing about with coats of arms. We ditched it in favour of the much better variant proposed by Sheldon Smith (EyeInSky).

RATING: 7.5
Interesting game with some elements reminiscent of El Grande and Shadow of the Emperor. Need a good understanding of all elements from the get go, since each bad decision may cost you a point or two, and with most Ruediger Dorn games, it appears scoring will be very tight. Felt that the luck of the shield draws/bonus was a bit overpowered given how close the rest of the scoring has been. I like it, but one poor move or bad decision can completely take you out of the running -- that's how important every single point is.

RATING: 7
For a medium-sized game Louis XIV sure pack quite a punch in terms of rules complexity.  On the one hand, it makes for a meaty and dense gameplay.  On the other, it's really a big hurdle to get through in order to enjoy all that it has to offer.  The area majority aspect combined with the neat influence marker placement often result in some intense battle from round to round, especially among the characters that reward the mission markers (almost too much sometimes, which cause the other 6 to seem weaker in comparison).  Unfortunately, the imbalance in the mission card powers, as well as the non-sensical coat of arms scoring, make the game seems "luckier" than it should be.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very satisfying game where you feel you are constantly doing things and you don't lose your time. This is a game with a small interaction that I like. As other Dorn's games Louis XIV is a very tactic game with simple rules. As most of others Dorn's games, the rulebook  is very convoluted and unthematic in consequence scares the new players. I find that the mechanics integrate into the theme but not completely. For example,  the two influence markers pools (private and general) and the mechanics associated to move them are counter intuitive; yes, it is to introduce the scarcity feeling, but thematically has no sense. Flipping the tile is an unthematic mechanic (for the purpose of variety). People complain about the complicated rules with some detail which unjustly reduces the ranking of this game. Games in the BGG top 50 have even more complicated, fiddly ans some others terrible rulebooks: War of the rings, Terra mystica, X-wing, Game of thrones.

RATING: 8
I am increasingly impressed with Rüdiger Dorn as a game designer.  Louis XIV provides enough "noise" in the game to keep the struggle for influence and benefits interesting in replays.

Also, thankfully, despite what I'd been told - "It's a game that takes 45 minutes to teach and 20 mintues to play" - it was untrue.  I read the rules, which may have taken 45 minutes, but then taught the game in 15 minutes.  I think the education would have gone better but slower if I'd instead just had us all play a face-up demo round.  Also, needless to say (since the game is rated at 75-100 minutes), Louis XIV did not take 20 minutes to play.  Perhaps I need a better ability to detect hyperbole.  ;^)
